	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Bill Shankly</h1>

<p><b>William Shankly</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Officer_of_the_Order_of_the_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Officer of the Order of the British Empire">OBE</a></span> (2 September 1913 – 29 September 1981) was a Scottish <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> player and <a href="/wiki/Manager_(association_football)" title="Manager (association football)">manager</a>, who is best known for his time as manager of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a>. Shankly brought success to Liverpool, gaining promotion to the First Division and winning three League Championships and the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Cup</a>. He laid foundations on which his successors <a href="/wiki/Bob_Paisley" title="Bob Paisley">Bob Paisley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Fagan" title="Joe Fagan">Joe Fagan</a> were able to build by winning seven league titles and four European Cups in the ten seasons after Shankly retired in 1974.
</p><p>Shankly came from a small Scottish mining community and was one of five brothers who played football professionally. He played as a ball-winning <a href="/wiki/Wing_half" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing half">right-half</a> and was capped twelve times for <a href="/wiki/Scotland_national_football_team" title="Scotland national football team">Scotland</a>, including seven wartime internationals. He spent one season at <a href="/wiki/Carlisle_United_F.C." title="Carlisle United F.C.">Carlisle United</a> before spending the rest of his career at <a href="/wiki/Preston_North_End_F.C." title="Preston North End F.C.">Preston North End</a>, with whom he won the <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/1938_FA_Cup_Final" title="1938 FA Cup Final">1938</a>. His playing career was interrupted by his service in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> during the Second World War. He became a manager after he retired from playing in 1949, returning to Carlisle United. He later managed <a href="/wiki/Grimsby_Town_F.C." title="Grimsby Town F.C.">Grimsby Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Workington_A.F.C." title="Workington A.F.C.">Workington</a><sup id="cite_ref-Allen_1990_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_1990-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allen_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Town_A.F.C." title="Huddersfield Town A.F.C.">Huddersfield Town</a> before moving to become Liverpool manager in December 1959.
</p><p>Shankly took charge of Liverpool when they were in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Second_Division" title="Football League Second Division">Second Division</a> and rebuilt the team into a major force in English and European football. He led Liverpool to the Second Division Championship to gain promotion to the top-flight <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a> in 1962, before going on to win three First Division Championships, two FA Cups, four <a href="/wiki/FA_Community_Shield" title="FA Community Shield">Charity Shields</a> and one <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Cup</a>. Shankly announced his surprise retirement from football a few weeks after Liverpool had won the <a href="/wiki/1974_FA_Cup_Final" title="1974 FA Cup Final">1974 FA Cup Final</a>, having managed the club for 15 years, and was succeeded by his long-time assistant <a href="/wiki/Bob_Paisley" title="Bob Paisley">Bob Paisley</a>. He led the Liverpool team out for the last time at Wembley for the <a href="/wiki/1974_FA_Charity_Shield" title="1974 FA Charity Shield">1974 FA Charity Shield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> He died seven years later, aged 68.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bill Shankly was born in the small coal mining village of <a href="/wiki/Glenbuck" title="Glenbuck">Glenbuck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayrshire" title="Ayrshire">Ayrshire</a>, whose population in 1913, the year of Shankly's birth, was around 700. People born there would often move to find work in larger coal mines.<sup id="cite_ref-S9_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S9-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Glenbuck became largely derelict and by the time Shankly's ghost writer John Roberts visited it in 1976, there were only 12 houses left, including a cottage owned by Shankly's sister, Elizabeth, whom Roberts described as "the last of the children of Glenbuck".<sup id="cite_ref-S9_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S9-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly's parents, John and Barbara, lived in one of the Auchenstilloch Cottages with their 10 children; five boys and five girls.<sup id="cite_ref-K12_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K12-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was the ninth child and the youngest boy.<sup id="cite_ref-K12_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K12-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Although he was known as Bill throughout his football career, his name in the family was Willie, pronounced [<i>Wullie</i>].<sup id="cite_ref-S21_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S21-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His father was a postman who became a tailor of handmade suits. Despite the football pedigree in his family, he did not play himself.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All five Shankly brothers played professional football and Shankly claimed they could have beaten any five brothers in the world when they were all at their peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-S9_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S9-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> His brothers were <a href="/wiki/Alec_Shankly" title="Alec Shankly">Alec</a>, known as Sandy by the family, who played for <a href="/wiki/Ayr_United_F.C." title="Ayr United F.C.">Ayr United</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clyde_F.C." title="Clyde F.C.">Clyde</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Shankly" title="Jimmy Shankly">Jimmy</a> (1902–1972), who played for various clubs including <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_United_F.C." title="Sheffield United F.C.">Sheffield United</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southend_United_F.C." title="Southend United F.C.">Southend United</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Shankly" title="John Shankly">John</a> (1903–1960), who played for <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_F.C." title="Portsmouth F.C.">Portsmouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luton_Town_F.C." title="Luton Town F.C.">Luton Town</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Shankly" title="Bob Shankly">Bob</a> (1910–1982), who played for <a href="/wiki/Alloa_Athletic_F.C." title="Alloa Athletic F.C.">Alloa Athletic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Falkirk_F.C." title="Falkirk F.C.">Falkirk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-K16_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K16-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Bob became a successful manager, guiding <a href="/wiki/Dundee_F.C." title="Dundee F.C.">Dundee</a> to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Football_League" title="Scottish Football League">Scottish championship</a> in 1962 and the semi-finals of the <a href="/wiki/European_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup">European Cup</a> the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-K16_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K16-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Their maternal uncles, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Blyth" title="Bob Blyth">Robert</a> and William Blyth were professional players who both became club directors at Portsmouth and <a href="/wiki/Carlisle_United_F.C." title="Carlisle United F.C.">Carlisle United</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-K12_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K12-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly wrote in his autobiography that times were hard during his upbringing and that hunger was a prevailing condition, especially during the winter months.<sup id="cite_ref-S13_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S13-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He admitted that he and his friends used to steal vegetables from nearby farms; bread, biscuits and fruit from suppliers' wagons, and bags of coal from the pits.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Shankly admitted the act was wrong but insisted it was done out of devilment only because the root cause was their constant hunger. He said that he and his friends learned from their mistakes and became better people in later years.<sup id="cite_ref-S14_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S14-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He was at school from age five until 14. Discipline at both home and school was strict but Shankly said it was character-building.<sup id="cite_ref-S15_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S15-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> His favourite subject was <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> and he played football as often as possible, especially in the school playground, but there was no organised school team.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Shankly left school in 1928, he worked at a local mine alongside his brother Bob. He did this for two years until the pit closed and he faced unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In his autobiography, he described the life of a miner at some length and mentioned many of the problems such as the sheer hard work, rats, the difficulties of eating and drinking at the coal face. The worst problem was the filth because the miners never felt really clean, even though they would go home to wash in a tub after each shift.<sup id="cite_ref-S18_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S18-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Shankly was employed as a miner, he played football as often as possible and sometimes went to <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> to watch either <a href="/wiki/Celtic_F.C." title="Celtic F.C.">Celtic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rangers_F.C." title="Rangers F.C.">Rangers</a>, sharing his allegiance between the two and ignoring the sectarianism that divides Glasgow.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Shankly developed his skills to the point that he was unemployed for only a few months before Carlisle United signed him. He wrote that he had his football future worked out in his mind and that, even when working in the pit, he was only "killing time". He always believed that it was only a matter of time before he became a professional player. He explained that, in football terms, he had always been an optimist with a belief in his destiny and that was the basis of his undying enthusiasm for the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-S19_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S19-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly's village team was called the <a href="/wiki/Glenbuck_Cherrypickers" class="mw-redirect" title="Glenbuck Cherrypickers">Glenbuck Cherrypickers</a>, a name probably derived from the <a href="/wiki/11th_Hussars" title="11th Hussars">11th Hussars</a> (the "Cherry Pickers"), but he said the club was near extinction when he had a trial and he never actually played for them.<sup id="cite_ref-S27_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S27-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Shankly, aged 18, then played part of the 1931–32 season for <a href="/wiki/Cronberry_Eglinton_F.C." title="Cronberry Eglinton F.C.">Cronberry Eglinton</a>, about 12 miles from Glenbuck. He used to cycle to and from the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-S27_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S27-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Cronberry were in the Cumnock &amp; District League.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Although Shankly had less than one full season at Cronberry, he acknowledged his debt to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Junior_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Junior League">Scottish Junior Football</a> as he learned a lot, mainly by listening to older players and especially his brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-S27_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S27-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Playing_career">Playing career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Playing career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carlisle_United">Carlisle United</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Carlisle United">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shankly had a single season, 1932–33, at Carlisle United, then relatively new to the <a href="/wiki/Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League">Football League</a> and playing in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Third_Division_North" title="Football League Third Division North">Third Division North</a>, their reserve side playing in the <a href="/wiki/North_Eastern_League" title="North Eastern League">North Eastern League</a>. Shankly was recommended by a scout called Peter Carruthers who had seen him playing for Cronberry.<sup id="cite_ref-S34_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S34-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He was invited for a month's trial and said it was the first time he had left Scotland. He was signed after just one trial match for Carlisle's reserves against <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_F.C." title="Middlesbrough F.C.">Middlesbrough reserves</a>, even though Carlisle reserves lost the match 6–0.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> A local newspaper report said that he had worked hard and might develop into a useful <a href="/wiki/Left_back" class="mw-redirect" title="Left back">left back</a>. In fact, he developed into a top-class <a href="/wiki/Wing_half" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing half">right-half</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly made his senior debut on 31 December 1932 in a 2–2 draw against <a href="/wiki/Rochdale_A.F.C." title="Rochdale A.F.C.">Rochdale</a> and made 16 appearances for the first team.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-CU_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-CU-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the season, the reserves won the North Eastern League Cup, defeating <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle United">Newcastle United</a> reserves 1–0 in the final. In his 1976 autobiography, Shankly stated that he still had the medal.<sup id="cite_ref-S35_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S35-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At this stage of his career, Shankly was assessed as "a hard running, gritty right-half"<sup id="cite_ref-wing_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wing-2">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> whose displays brought him much praise and credit. He was considered a promising key young player who was capable of taking Carlisle to greater things.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-CU_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-CU-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> He was paid four <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pounds</a> ten <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_coin)" title="Shilling (British coin)">shillings</a> a week at Carlisle which he considered a good wage as the top rate at that time was eight pounds.<sup id="cite_ref-K21_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K21-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was happy at Carlisle which was close to his home at Glenbuck and he had settled in well with almost a guarantee of first team football. When the opportunity came for him to move on, he was not convinced he wanted to leave.<sup id="cite_ref-K23_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K23-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preston_North_End">Preston North End</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Preston North End">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soon after the 1932–33 season ended, Shankly received a telegram from Carlisle United asking him to return as soon as possible because another club wanted to sign him.<sup id="cite_ref-S37_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S37-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Arriving at Carlisle, he discovered that the interested club was <a href="/wiki/Preston_North_End_F.C." title="Preston North End F.C.">Preston North End</a> who had offered a transfer fee of £500. The terms for Shankly personally were a fee of £50 plus a £10 signing-on fee and wages of five pounds a week.<sup id="cite_ref-K23_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K23-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Shankly's initial reaction was that it was not enough and the deal nearly fell through.<sup id="cite_ref-K23_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K23-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> His brother Alec pointed out to him that Preston were in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Second_Division" title="Football League Second Division">Second Division</a> and a bigger club than Carlisle with the potential to regain <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a> status.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Alec persuaded him that the opportunity was more important than what he would be paid immediately, stressing that it was what he would get later that counted. Shankly took his brother's advice and signed the Preston contract in a railway carriage.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly began his Preston career in the reserves, who played in the <a href="/wiki/Central_League_(football)" class="mw-redirect" title="Central League (football)">Central League</a> which was a higher standard than the North Eastern League.<sup id="cite_ref-S39_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S39-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He made his first team debut on 9 December 1933, three months after his 20th birthday, against <a href="/wiki/Hull_City_A.F.C." title="Hull City A.F.C.">Hull City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Shankly created an early goal to help Preston win 5–0, earning him praise in a national newspaper for his "clever passing".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> With his wholehearted attitude and commitment to the team, he quickly established himself as a first-team regular and became a crowd favourite.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Preston fulfilled their potential and gained promotion to the First Division as runners-up to <a href="/wiki/Grimsby_Town_F.C." title="Grimsby Town F.C.">Grimsby Town</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It was therefore a successful debut season for Shankly who stayed with Preston until he retired in 1949. His wage was increased to eight pounds a week with six pounds in the summer.<sup id="cite_ref-S40_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S40-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In a summary of the 1933–34 season, a Preston correspondent, Walter Pilkington, wrote:<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>One of this season's discoveries, Bill Shankly, played with rare tenacity and uncommonly good ideas for a lad of 20. He is full of good football and possessed with unlimited energy; he should go far.</p></blockquote>
<p>In his autobiography, Shankly wrote that Preston had more than held their own in the 1934–35 season and the club was not relegated again until the end of the 1948–49 season in which he left them.<sup id="cite_ref-S40_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S40-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Shankly developed into a tough half back, as good as any in the Football League.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The outstanding <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Northern Ireland national football team">Northern Ireland</a> international <a href="/wiki/Peter_Doherty_(footballer)" title="Peter Doherty (footballer)">Peter Doherty</a> recalled how Shankly dogged his footsteps in one match and kept muttering: "Great wee team, North End, great wee team", subduing Doherty completely as Preston defeated <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a> 3–1.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1936–37, Preston reached the <a href="/wiki/1937_FA_Cup_Final" title="1937 FA Cup Final">FA Cup Final</a> but were well beaten 3–1 by <a href="/wiki/Sunderland_A.F.C." title="Sunderland A.F.C.">Sunderland</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium_(1923)" title="Wembley Stadium (1923)">Wembley Stadium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S41_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S41-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Preston recovered to reach the <a href="/wiki/1938_FA_Cup_Final" title="1938 FA Cup Final">1938 FA Cup Final</a> in which they defeated <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Town_A.F.C." title="Huddersfield Town A.F.C.">Huddersfield Town</a> 1–0 with a penalty scored by <a href="/wiki/George_Mutch" title="George Mutch">George Mutch</a> in the final minute of extra time. As well as winning the FA Cup, Preston finished third in the league.<sup id="cite_ref-S41_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S41-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> That season marked the pinnacle of Shankly's playing career.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly had just reached his 26th birthday when the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> began and the war claimed the peak years of his playing career.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He joined the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) and managed to play in numerous wartime league, cup and exhibition matches for <a href="/wiki/Norwich_City_F.C." title="Norwich City F.C.">Norwich City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, Luton Town and <a href="/wiki/Partick_Thistle_F.C." title="Partick Thistle F.C.">Partick Thistle</a>, depending on where he was stationed.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> On 30 May 1942, he played a single game for <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a> in a 4–1 win over <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> at <a href="/wiki/Anfield" title="Anfield">Anfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was keen on <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a> and fought as a <a href="/wiki/Middleweight" title="Middleweight">middleweight</a> in the RAF, winning a trophy when he was stationed in Manchester.<sup id="cite_ref-S52_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S52-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He confirmed in his autobiography that his weight as an RAF boxer was 159 pounds (72&#160;kg) and he was only six pounds (2.7&#160;kg) heavier than that in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-S52_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S52-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Shankly met his wife, Nessie, in the RAF (she was in the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Auxiliary_Air_Force" title="Women&#39;s Auxiliary Air Force">WAAF</a> and stationed at the same camp) and they married in 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the resumption of full League football again in the 1946–47 season, Shankly returned to Preston who held his registration, but he was now 33 and coming to the end of his playing days.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> By 1949, he was Preston's club captain but had lost his place in the first team, which was struggling against relegation despite having <a href="/wiki/Tom_Finney" title="Tom Finney">Tom Finney</a> in the side.<sup id="cite_ref-K57_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K57-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was a qualified <a href="/wiki/Masseur" class="mw-redirect" title="Masseur">masseur</a> and had decided he wanted to become a coach so, when Carlisle United asked him to become their manager in March of that year, he retired as a player and accepted the job.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Shankly's departure from Preston was resented by some at the club and he was refused a benefit match, to which he felt entitled. He described Preston's attitude as the biggest let-down of his life in football.<sup id="cite_ref-S60_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S60-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly had enormous admiration for Tom Finney and devotes more than three pages of his autobiography to Finney's prowess as a footballer.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In the 1970s, Shankly was asked how a current star compared to Finney and Shankly replied: "Aye, he's as good as Tommy – but then Tommy's nearly 60 now".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Another Preston player admired by Shankly was his Scottish international teammate <a href="/wiki/Andy_Beattie" title="Andy Beattie">Andy Beattie</a>, with whom he would later work in management.<sup id="cite_ref-S47_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S47-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was succeeded in the Preston team by <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Docherty" title="Tommy Docherty">Tommy Docherty</a> and Shankly told Docherty that he should just put the number four shirt on and let it run round by itself because it knows where to go.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-PNE_36-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-PNE-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shankly played for Scotland 12 times from 1938 to 1943 in five full and seven wartime internationals.<sup id="cite_ref-S6_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S6-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He spoke of his "unbelievable pride" when playing for Scotland against <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> and how, when confronted by the "<a href="/wiki/Auld_enemy" class="mw-redirect" title="Auld enemy">Auld Enemy</a>", the Scottish players would become <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> or <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a> for 90 minutes after pulling on the blue jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-S20_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S20-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Shankly himself certainly had that spirit when playing for Scotland as confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Alex_James_(footballer)" title="Alex James (footballer)">Alex James</a>, who said of Shankly: "He is a real Scotland player who will fight until he drops".<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly made his international debut on 9 April 1938 against England at Wembley; Scotland winning the match 1–0 with a late goal by <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Walker_(footballer,_born_1915)" title="Tommy Walker (footballer, born 1915)">Tommy Walker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nine of his Scotland appearances were against England and the others were against Northern Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Wales_national_football_team" title="Wales national football team">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungary_national_football_team" title="Hungary national football team">Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> He was Scotland's captain in the wartime match against England at <a href="/wiki/Hampden_Park" title="Hampden Park">Hampden Park</a>, attended by 78,000 people on 3 May 1941, but Scotland lost that game 1–3.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps his most memorable international was the wartime game at Wembley on 18 April 1942 when Scotland won 5–4 and Shankly scored his only Scotland goal. A post-match report said that Scotland's success was inspired by "the Busby–Shankly victory service" when Shankly and his future management rival <a href="/wiki/Matt_Busby" title="Matt Busby">Matt Busby</a> combined to help Scotland's cause.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> According to the Liverpool website, Shankly's goal was "probably the strangest national goal ever".<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> He took a speculative shot towards goal from 50 yards and the ball bounced over the England goalkeeper's head and into the net.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-SCO_52-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-SCO-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In January 1973, when Shankly was the subject of <i><a href="/wiki/This_Is_Your_Life_(UK_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="This Is Your Life (UK TV series)">This Is Your Life</a></i>, the goal was shown and Shankly commented that "they all count and we won".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_technique">Style and technique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Style and technique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shankly declared in his autobiography that he specialised in what he called "the art of tackling", emphasising that it is an art.<sup id="cite_ref-S31_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He wrote that he was never sent off or booked by a referee.<sup id="cite_ref-S31_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In his view, the art of tackling is in the timing and the sole object is to win the ball. He wrote that even if the opponent is injured in the tackle, it is not a foul if you have timed everything right and you've won the ball.<sup id="cite_ref-S31_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> His philosophy, therefore, was to play hard but fair with no cheating.<sup id="cite_ref-S31_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> During his playing career, Shankly said he would not argue with referees. He realised after taking the advice of his brothers that it is a waste of time. The referee, he wrote, always wins in the end.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly was always noted for his dedication to football and, in his playing days, would do his own training during the summer months. During the summer of 1933 when he returned to Glenbuck after completing his first season as a professional, he decided to develop his <a href="/wiki/Throw-in" title="Throw-in">throw-in</a> skills. He was an early exponent of the long throw-in - he practised by throwing balls over a row of houses and the small boys of the village helped by fetching them back for him.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-CU_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-CU-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management_career">Management career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Management career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shankly wrote in his autobiography that he had long prepared himself for a career as a football manager.<sup id="cite_ref-S60_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S60-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> He had absorbed all the coaching systems with any useful qualifications and had full confidence in his ability and in himself to be a leader. For him, he had done the hard work and it was simply a question of waiting for an opportunity to present itself.<sup id="cite_ref-S60_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S60-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Shankly summed up the essential criteria for success in football management when he claimed he could speak common sense about the game and could spot a good player.<sup id="cite_ref-S61_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S61-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In spotting a player, he always applied a basic formula which was that, first and foremost, the player must have both ability and courage. Other attributes were physical fitness and willingness to work, especially to struggle against the odds.<sup id="cite_ref-S61_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S61-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carlisle_United_2">Carlisle United</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Carlisle United">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shankly began his managerial career at the club where his professional playing career had started. Carlisle in the 1948–49 season were struggling in the bottom half of the Third Division North and finding it difficult to attract southern-based players because of the town's geographic remoteness in the far north of England.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-MCU_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-MCU-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Shankly's work ethic transformed the team who finished 15th in 1948–49 after he had been in charge for only the last few matches. They improved to ninth in 1949–50 and then to third in 1950–51, almost gaining promotion.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-MCU_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-MCU-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Shankly's players at Carlisle was <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Twentyman" title="Geoff Twentyman">Geoff Twentyman</a>, then a promising young <a href="/wiki/Centre_half" class="mw-redirect" title="Centre half">centre half</a>, who was later transferred to Liverpool. After he retired from playing, Twentyman became chief scout at Liverpool, working with Shankly and finding several outstandingly talented players.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly used <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> to motivate his players, for example telling them that the opposition had had a very tiring journey and were not fit to play the match. He urged the local population to support the team and would use the public address system at matches to tell the crowd about his team changes and how his strategy was improving the team.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-MCU_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-MCU-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Shankly recalled that <a href="/wiki/Brunton_Park" title="Brunton Park">Brunton Park</a> was dilapidated, writing that the main stand was falling to pieces and the terraces derelict.<sup id="cite_ref-S62_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S62-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He even burned all the kit. When the team was travelling to <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_City_F.C." title="Lincoln City F.C.">Lincoln City</a>, he saw a sportswear shop in <a href="/wiki/Doncaster" title="Doncaster">Doncaster</a> and stopped the coach to buy a full set of kit in which the team played at Lincoln.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Season ticket sales in 1950–51 reached an all-time high and Carlisle challenged strongly for promotion as well as achieving a draw with Arsenal at <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_Stadium" title="Arsenal Stadium">Highbury</a> in the FA Cup. It ended badly, however, because Shankly accused the club's board of reneging on a bonus promise for the players should the team finish in the top three of the league. He resigned and accepted an offer from Grimsby Town. Shankly's overall record in league football at Carlisle was 42 wins and 22 defeats from 95 matches.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-MCU_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-MCU-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grimsby_Town">Grimsby Town</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Grimsby Town">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After an unsuccessful interview at Liverpool,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Shankly moved to manage Grimsby Town in June 1951. He said in his autobiography that there was greater potential at Grimsby than at Carlisle.<sup id="cite_ref-S66_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S66-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> His main problems were that Grimsby had been relegated twice in recent seasons, dropping from the First to the Third Division, and some good players had been transferred before he arrived.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Even so, Shankly believed he still had good players to work with and was able to buy some additional players on the transfer market for low fees.<sup id="cite_ref-S66_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S66-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Grimsby made a strong challenge for promotion in 1951–52 but finished second, three points behind Lincoln City (only one team was promoted from Division Three North, with one from Division Three South).<sup id="cite_ref-S67_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S67-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Shankly insisted in his autobiography that his Grimsby team was:<sup id="cite_ref-S67_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S67-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Pound for pound, and class for class, the best football team I have seen in England since the war. In the league they were in, they played football nobody else could play. Everything was measured, planned and perfected and you could not wish to see more entertaining football.</p></blockquote>
<p>Shankly's biographer <a href="/wiki/Stephen_F._Kelly" title="Stephen F. Kelly">Stephen Kelly</a> quotes the "pound for pound" analysis but qualifies it as another of Shankly's slight, though well-meant, exaggerations.<sup id="cite_ref-K77_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K77-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Kelly added that this kind of talk by Shankly could only boost morale at the club.<sup id="cite_ref-K77_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K77-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly made great use of five-a-side football in training at Grimsby, playing these games as if they were competitive cup or league matches.<sup id="cite_ref-S68_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S68-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The games would last an hour each time. Shankly worked on set pieces such as throw-ins and tried to devise a method of counter-attacking from corners conceded.<sup id="cite_ref-S68_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S68-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Grimsby's ageing team made a bright start in 1952–53 with five straight wins but eventually slipped and finished in fifth place.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 1953–54, Shankly became disillusioned when the board could not give him money to buy new players. He was reluctant to promote some promising reserves because of loyalty to the older players (a fault that was to resurface at Liverpool years later) and he finally resigned in January 1954, citing the board's lack of ambition as his main reason.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-GT_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-GT-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In his autobiography, he said that he and his wife were feeling homesick in Grimsby and, when an opportunity came to manage <a href="/wiki/Workington_A.F.C." title="Workington A.F.C.">Workington</a>, he was attracted to the challenge partly because they would be closer to Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-S68_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S68-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Shankly's record in league football at Grimsby was 62 wins and 35 defeats from 118 matches.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-GT_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-GT-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Workington">Workington</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Workington">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the Workington team was close to the bottom of the Third Division North, Shankly saw it as a challenge and attacked the job with all of his usual enthusiasm and relish.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-W_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-W-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Workington rose to 18th by the end of the 1953–54 season and so did not have to apply for re-election. In 1954–55, the team finished a creditable eighth and saw a rise in attendances from 6,000 to 8,000.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-W_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-W-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Workington operated on a shoestring and Shankly had to do much of the administration work himself, including answering the telephone and dealing with the mail by using an old typewriter to answer letters.<sup id="cite_ref-Allen_1990_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_1990-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allen_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In addition, he had the risky job of going to the bank each week to collect the payroll. One of his main problems was sharing the ground with the <a href="/wiki/Workington_Town" title="Workington Town">local rugby league club</a> and Shankly was very concerned about the damage done to the playing surface by the rugby players.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The situation led to numerous arguments with the club's board which, as Kelly records, included a majority of rugby league men whose interest in football took second place to rugby.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly resigned on 15 November 1955 to take up the post of assistant manager at Huddersfield Town, working with his old friend Andy Beattie.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> His record in league football at Workington was 35 wins and 27 defeats from 85 matches.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-W_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-W-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Huddersfield_Town">Huddersfield Town</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Huddersfield Town">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shankly's initial role at Huddersfield was as reserve team coach. He found himself in charge of several promising youngsters who soon graduated to the first team after Town were relegated to the Second Division at the end of the 1955–56 season. Beattie resigned in the next season and, on 5 November 1956, Shankly succeeded him as manager. On Christmas Eve, he gave a first team debut to 16-year-old prospect <a href="/wiki/Denis_Law" title="Denis Law">Denis Law</a>. Another prospect in his team was left back <a href="/wiki/Ray_Wilson_(English_footballer)" title="Ray Wilson (English footballer)">Ray Wilson</a> who went on to become Huddersfield's most capped player before joining Everton.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Shankly did not gain promotion at Huddersfield, the team finishing 12th in 1956–57, ninth in 1957–58 and 14th in 1958–59.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-HT_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-HT-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other players in Shankly's Huddersfield team were <a href="/wiki/Ken_Taylor_(cricketer,_born_1935)" title="Ken Taylor (cricketer, born 1935)">Ken Taylor</a>, who was an <a href="/wiki/England_national_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="England national cricket team">England</a> <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test cricketer</a>; striker <a href="/wiki/Les_Massie" title="Les Massie">Les Massie</a> and captain <a href="/wiki/Bill_McGarry" title="Bill McGarry">Bill McGarry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S75_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S75-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> On 21 December 1957, Huddersfield lost 7–6 to <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Athletic_F.C." title="Charlton Athletic F.C.">Charlton Athletic</a>, who played most of the match with ten men, after Huddersfield were leading 5–1 with just 27 minutes remaining. Shankly described it as one of the most amazing games he had ever seen.<sup id="cite_ref-S82_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S82-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> On another occasion, Huddersfield beat Liverpool 5–0 with ten men and Shankly recalled the Liverpool directors leaving the ground in single file as if they were in a funeral procession.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Disillusioned by a board that wanted to sell his best players without offering money to buy replacements, Shankly felt stifled by Huddersfield's lack of ambition and was delighted in November 1959 to receive an approach for his services by Liverpool. He recalled how Liverpool chairman Tom (T.V.) Williams asked him if he would like to manage the best club in the country, to which Shankly replied: "Why, is Matt Busby packing up?"<sup id="cite_ref-S84_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S84-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Shankly decided to think about the offer as he realised the great potential at Liverpool, who like Huddersfield were in the Second Division at that time. Rumours began and were fuelled by Liverpool's visit to <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Road" title="Leeds Road">Leeds Road</a> on 28 November. Although Huddersfield won the game 1–0, Shankly accepted the Liverpool offer and resigned his position as Huddersfield manager at a board meeting on 1 December 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> His league record at Huddersfield was 49 wins and 47 defeats in 129 matches.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-HT_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-HT-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liverpool">Liverpool</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Liverpool">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Early_years_.281959.E2.80.931964.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_(1959–1964)">Early years (1959–1964)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Early years (1959–1964)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When Shankly arrived at Anfield on Monday, 14 December 1959, Liverpool had been in the Second Division for five years, and had been defeated by non-league <a href="/wiki/Worcester_City_F.C." title="Worcester City F.C.">Worcester City</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1958%E2%80%9359_FA_Cup" title="1958–59 FA Cup">1958–59 FA Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Anfield itself was in disrepair with no means of watering the pitch and Shankly insisted the club spend £3,000 to rectify that.<sup id="cite_ref-S85_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S85-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Shankly described the training ground at <a href="/wiki/Melwood" title="Melwood">Melwood</a> as "a shambles".<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Liverpool squad he inherited consisted largely of average players and some promising reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In spite of the difficulties, Shankly felt immediately at home in his new club and he believed that he shared an immediate bond with the supporters, who he saw as his kind of people.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> He quickly established working relationships with the coaching staff of <a href="/wiki/Bob_Paisley" title="Bob Paisley">Bob Paisley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Fagan" title="Joe Fagan">Joe Fagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reuben_Bennett" title="Reuben Bennett">Reuben Bennett</a> who shared his views about loyalty to each other and to the club.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Paisley's influence at Liverpool was crucial for, as Kelly puts it, Shankly was "the great motivating force behind Liverpool, but it was Paisley who was the tactician".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> One aspect of the quartet's legacy to football was the conversion of an old storage room into what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Boot_Room" class="mw-redirect" title="Boot Room">Boot Room</a>", which was used for tactical discussion while cleaning and repairing boots.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Although Shankly believed he had an excellent coaching staff, the playing staff were not so impressive – indeed, Shankly said of the latter:<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>After only one match I knew that the team as a whole was not good enough. I made up my mind that we needed strengthening through the middle, a goalkeeper and a centre half who between them could stop goals, and somebody up front to create goals and score them.</p></blockquote>
<p>To deal with what he saw as a below average playing squad, he placed 24 players on the transfer list. All of them had left the club within one year. Shankly resolutely pursued his strength through the middle goal and always knew which three players he needed to achieve it.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Melwood was overgrown and had only an old wooden <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> pavilion.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Shankly commented that one of the pitches looked as if bombs had been dropped on it and he asked if the Germans had been over in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-S87_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S87-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> He instituted a development programme to cultivate the site and modernise the facilities. In the meantime, he arranged for the players to meet and change at Anfield before going to and from Melwood by bus.<sup id="cite_ref-S87_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S87-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> According to Kelly, Liverpool's eventual success was based on hard work done in the training system that Shankly, Paisley, Fagan and Bennett introduced at Melwood. Shankly deplored long distance running on roads and insisted that, apart from warm-up exercises or any special exercises needed to overcome injuries, the players trained on grass using a ball. Everything was done systematically with players rotating through exercise routines in groups with the purpose of achieving set targets. Five-a-side games, as at all Shankly's earlier clubs, were at the heart of the system and he again insisted on these being as competitive as league matches.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> One particular routine designed to develop stamina, reflexes and ball skills was the "sweat box" which Shankly described as: "using boards like the walls of a house with players playing the ball off one wall and on to the next; the ball was played against the boards, you controlled it, turned around and took it again".<sup id="cite_ref-S96_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S96-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Shankly got the idea from a routine he had seen Tom Finney use at Preston to hone his skills. After experimenting with the routine, he set the players a limit of two minutes per session. The system was geared to Shankly's simple philosophy of "pass and move", which formed the basis of Liverpool's strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Shankly insisted on suitable cooling-off periods after training (now called "warming down") before the players took a bath and had a meal. The team changed the studs in their boots to suit all playing conditions. Shankly summarised the entire strategy in terms of attention to detail with nothing left to chance.<sup id="cite_ref-S100_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S100-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool's recovery depended on new players being acquired and, in his autobiography, Shankly recalled the struggles he had with the board to make them realise the club's potential and the need to spend money on good players. He said there were times when he felt like walking out. He found a valuable ally in Eric Sawyer, of the <a href="/wiki/Littlewoods" title="Littlewoods">Littlewoods</a> pools organisation, who joined the board not long after Shankly's appointment and shared Shankly's vision of Liverpool as the best club in England. At one board meeting in 1961 when Shankly insisted the club make offers for two players in Scotland, the board's initial response was that they couldn't afford them, but Sawyer stepped in and said: "We can't afford <i>not</i> to buy them".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two Scottish players were centre half <a href="/wiki/Ron_Yeats" title="Ron Yeats">Ron Yeats</a> and centre forward <a href="/wiki/Ian_St_John" title="Ian St John">Ian St John</a> from <a href="/wiki/Dundee_United_F.C." title="Dundee United F.C.">Dundee United</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motherwell_F.C." title="Motherwell F.C.">Motherwell</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> With Sawyer's help, Shankly signed them both in the spring of 1961 and challenged the Liverpool board to sack him if they couldn't play.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> At a press conference when Yeats came to Liverpool, Shankly emphasised Yeats' height by inviting the journalists to "go and walk round him; he's a colossus!"<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Lawrence" title="Tommy Lawrence">Tommy Lawrence</a> came through the club's junior teams, so Shankly now had his "strength through the middle" and the team building continued with the acquisition of wing half <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Milne" title="Gordon Milne">Gordon Milne</a> from Preston. Other players developed at Anfield were <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Melia" title="Jimmy Melia">Jimmy Melia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Moran" title="Ronnie Moran">Ronnie Moran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_A%27Court" title="Alan A&#39;Court">Alan A'Court</a> and the future England internationals <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Byrne_(footballer,_born_1938)" title="Gerry Byrne (footballer, born 1938)">Gerry Byrne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Hunt" title="Roger Hunt">Roger Hunt</a>. Shankly said of goalscorer Hunt the first time he saw him: "Christ, this one can play!"<sup id="cite_ref-S91_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S91-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Liverpool had finished third in both 1959–60 and 1960–61 (only the top two clubs were promoted); but the new team gained promotion in the 1961–62 season by winning the Second Division championship, Hunt scoring 41 goals.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool consolidated in 1962–63, finishing eighth in their first top flight season under Shankly's management. Significant signings were wing half <a href="/wiki/Willie_Stevenson" title="Willie Stevenson">Willie Stevenson</a> from Rangers in 1962 and left winger <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thompson_(footballer,_born_1942)" title="Peter Thompson (footballer, born 1942)">Peter Thompson</a> from Preston in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Shankly described the Thompson deal as "daylight robbery".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Liverpool's youth system produced more future England internationals in <a href="/wiki/Ian_Callaghan" title="Ian Callaghan">Ian Callaghan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Smith_(footballer,_born_1945)" title="Tommy Smith (footballer, born 1945)">Tommy Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Lawler" title="Chris Lawler">Chris Lawler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The hard work paid off in 1963–64 when Liverpool won their sixth League Championship title, ending the season with a 5–0 defeat of Arsenal at Anfield.<sup id="cite_ref-S94_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S94-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> According to Roger Hunt, the secret of Liverpool's success was that, under Shankly, they were the fittest team in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In 1964, Jimmy Melia was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton_Wanderers" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolverhampton Wanderers">Wolverhampton Wanderers</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> with Shankly buying Arsenal's utility player <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Strong" title="Geoff Strong">Geoff Strong</a> for £40,000 and this was Liverpool's last significant transfer activity until 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1964_to_1970">1964 to 1970</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1964 to 1970">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One of Shankly's greatest ambitions when he joined Liverpool was to win the FA Cup and, after he signed Yeats and St John, he told the club directors that they would win it with these two players in the team.<sup id="cite_ref-S91_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S91-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> It was St John who scored the winning goal in May 1965 when Liverpool did win the FA Cup for the first time in the club's history with a 2–1 extra time victory over <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United_F.C." title="Leeds United F.C.">Leeds United</a> at Wembley. In his autobiography, Shankly recounted that among his many achievements, winning the 1965 FA Cup final was his greatest day in football.<sup id="cite_ref-S102_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S102-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool made their European debut in 1964–65, competing in the European Cup and reaching the semi-finals. In the second round, the club was drawn against the formidable <a href="/wiki/R.S.C._Anderlecht" title="R.S.C. Anderlecht">Anderlecht</a>. Immediately before the match, Shankly decided to experiment with the Liverpool kit. Liverpool played in red shirts with white shorts and white socks with red tops but Shankly and Ian St John had the idea of an all-red kit that would give the impression the players were taller. Liverpool played in all-red only for European matches but quickly adopted the colour permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Three days after winning the FA Cup, Liverpool defeated European champions <a href="/wiki/F.C._Internazionale_Milano" class="mw-redirect" title="F.C. Internazionale Milano">Internazionale</a> 3–1 at Anfield in the semi-final first leg with a performance that was saluted by Inter's coach <a href="/wiki/Helenio_Herrera" title="Helenio Herrera">Helenio Herrera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The second leg at the <a href="/wiki/San_Siro" title="San Siro">San Siro</a> remains controversial because, according to Shankly, the match was "a war" which Liverpool lost 3–0 and so were knocked out of the competition 4–3 on aggregate.<sup id="cite_ref-S105_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S105-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Eleven years later, Shankly maintained that two of Inter's goals were illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-S106_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S106-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Even today, the Liverpool website describes the match by saying that Liverpool were denied at the semi-final stage by a dishonest referee in Milan.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> According to Kelly, however, video evidence shows that the two disputed goals were actually legitimate.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Shankly said after the defeat in Milan that the Inter fans were going mad because they were so pleased to have beaten Liverpool and he insisted it proved the high standard to which the Liverpool team had raised itself.<sup id="cite_ref-S106_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S106-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1964–65 Football League Championship, Liverpool dropped from first to seventh with 13 fewer points than the previous season, perhaps due to the exertion of lengthy participation in the FA and European Cups.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1965–66, Liverpool regained the League Championship title and reached the final of the <a href="/wiki/European_Cup_Winners%27_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup Winners&#39; Cup">European Cup Winners' Cup</a> at Hampden Park, where they lost 2–1 in extra time to <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a>. Shankly's summary of that final, played on a wet night, was that the team didn't play well and gave away "two silly goals".<sup id="cite_ref-S106_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S106-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Shankly and Paisley had learned a great deal about European football which Liverpool would eventually turn into trophies. Their strategy in two-legged ties would be containment away and attack at home.<sup id="cite_ref-K217_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K217-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Shankly had applied the principle in a preliminary round tie against <a href="/wiki/Juventus_F.C." title="Juventus F.C.">Juventus</a> when Liverpool were away in the first leg. Despite Juventus taking the lead after 81 minutes, Shankly ordered his players to ensure the deficit was only one goal. Liverpool then switched to all-out attack in the second leg at Anfield and won 2–0 (2–1 aggregate).<sup id="cite_ref-K217_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K217-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool began the 1966–67 season by beating neighbours Everton in the <a href="/wiki/FA_Community_Shield" title="FA Community Shield">FA Charity Shield</a> match but the team were never really in contention for major honours that season, finishing fifth in the league. Shankly recognised the potential of Blackpool teenager <a href="/wiki/Emlyn_Hughes" title="Emlyn Hughes">Emlyn Hughes</a>, a future England captain, and signed him for £65,000 in February 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-S106_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S106-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Liverpool's performance in the <a href="/wiki/1966%E2%80%9367_European_Cup" title="1966–67 European Cup">1966–67 European Cup</a> was poor and, after struggling to overcome <a href="/wiki/FC_Petrolul_Ploie%C8%99ti" title="FC Petrolul Ploiești">FC Petrolul Ploiești</a> in the first round, they were well beaten in the last 16 by <a href="/wiki/AFC_Ajax" title="AFC Ajax">Ajax Amsterdam</a>, inspired by 19-year-old <a href="/wiki/Johan_Cruyff" title="Johan Cruyff">Johan Cruyff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S107_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S107-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Ajax won 7–3 on aggregate after defeating Liverpool 5–1 in Amsterdam. Several years later, in his autobiography, Shankly still complained that the match in Amsterdam should never have started because of fog and, although Liverpool lost 5–1, he still thought Liverpool would win the tie at Anfield.<sup id="cite_ref-S107_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S107-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Although Shankly claimed to have been unworried about the Ajax defeat, he acknowledged that he was examining the team and planning ahead.<sup id="cite_ref-S108_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S108-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Liverpool site argues that Shankly was mistaken in his decision at this time to postpone team rebuilding.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L60_83-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L60-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool improved their league performances over the next two years, finishing third in 1967–68 and then second in 1968–69, although to Shankly himself it was "a mediocre time in the late 1960s as we prepared for the 1970s".<sup id="cite_ref-S109_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S109-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Shankly made two controversial signings in this period which did not turn out as he had hoped. In 1967, he signed striker <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hateley" title="Tony Hateley">Tony Hateley</a> from <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a> for a club record £96,000 and then felt obliged to transfer him to <a href="/wiki/Coventry_City_F.C." title="Coventry City F.C.">Coventry City</a> only a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-S109_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S109-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> He wrote that bad luck and injuries disrupted the progress of two other prospects <a href="/wiki/Alf_Arrowsmith" title="Alf Arrowsmith">Alf Arrowsmith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Wallace_(footballer,_born_1944)" title="Gordon Wallace (footballer, born 1944)">Gordon Wallace</a>; Shankly had compared the latter to Tom Finney.<sup id="cite_ref-S110_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S110-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In September 1968, he paid £100,000 to Wolverhampton Wanderers for their teenage striker <a href="/wiki/Alun_Evans" title="Alun Evans">Alun Evans</a> who thus became "football's first £100,000 teenager". Evans started well and produced some outstanding performances during his four years at the club but Shankly eventually had to sell him to <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">Aston Villa</a>. He later recalled that Evans was scarred by a glass in a nightclub incident which, in Shankly's opinion, had a detrimental impact on his career.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only long-term success that Shankly had in the transfer market in the late 1960s was his signing of Emlyn Hughes, who went on to captain Liverpool to victory in the European Cup. Otherwise, he did not significantly change the team until the 1969–70 season when Liverpool finished fifth in the league championship, a long way behind the winners, their local rivals Everton.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L70_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L70-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was characteristically defiant whenever Everton got the better of Liverpool and, although he liked and respected everyone connected with Everton, would always talk up Liverpool at Everton's expense. Typical of this was his joke about the city having two great football teams – Liverpool and Liverpool reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-DMQ_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DMQ-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1969–70 FA Cup quarter final, Liverpool played against struggling Second Division side <a href="/wiki/Watford_F.C." title="Watford F.C.">Watford</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vicarage_Road" title="Vicarage Road">Vicarage Road</a> and lost 1–0 after a very poor performance.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L70_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L70-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The Liverpool site records that the defeat signalled the end for St John, Hunt, Byrne, Yeats and Lawrence; the incomers included <a href="/wiki/Ray_Clemence" title="Ray Clemence">Ray Clemence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Lindsay" title="Alec Lindsay">Alec Lindsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Lloyd" title="Larry Lloyd">Larry Lloyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Toshack" title="John Toshack">John Toshack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Hall_(footballer,_born_1946)" title="Brian Hall (footballer, born 1946)">Brian Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Heighway" title="Steve Heighway">Steve Heighway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L70_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L70-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Apart from Hall who graduated through the reserves, they were all signed from clubs in lower divisions or even, in the case of Heighway, from non-league football.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Adding the new players to Tommy Smith, Ian Callaghan, Chris Lawler and Emlyn Hughes, Shankly formed the nucleus of a second great team which went on to dominate English and European football in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-L70_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-L70-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the new players came to Liverpool because of a new scouting system created by Shankly in 1967 and placed under the control of new chief scout Geoff Twentyman, who had played for Shankly at Carlisle and had then spent several seasons at Liverpool, retiring shortly before Shankly's appointment. According to Stephen Kelly, hiring Twentyman as chief scout was "perhaps Shankly's finest signing ever".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> It was through Twentyman that Liverpool found the new players and, after Shankly retired, Twentyman gave sterling service to Bob Paisley and Joe Fagan by finding players including <a href="/wiki/Phil_Neal" title="Phil Neal">Phil Neal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Hansen" title="Alan Hansen">Alan Hansen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ian_Rush" title="Ian Rush">Ian Rush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-K245_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K245-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> As always, Shankly kept things simple and Twentyman was told to look for a prospect's basic qualities which were the abilities to pass the ball and move into position to receive a pass.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Shankly also wanted Twentyman to check the player's personality and ensure he had the right attitude for a professional footballer. Above all, said Twentyman, "he wanted to know if the lad had the heart to play for Liverpool".<sup id="cite_ref-K245_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K245-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Although Shankly sometimes paid large transfer fees he was loath to do so and Twentyman's brief was to find young players so he (Shankly) could mould them into what he wanted.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1970_to_1974">1970 to 1974</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1970 to 1974">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The new team began promisingly in 1970–71 by retaining fifth place in the league and reaching the semi-finals of the <a href="/wiki/1970%E2%80%9371_Inter-Cities_Fairs_Cup" title="1970–71 Inter-Cities Fairs Cup">Inter-Cities Fairs Cup</a> where they lost 0–1 to a very experienced Leeds United side.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The highlight of Liverpool's European campaign that season was a 4–1 aggregate victory in the quarter-final over <a href="/wiki/FC_Bayern_Munich" title="FC Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> For the first time since winning the competition in 1965, Liverpool reached the <a href="/wiki/1971_FA_Cup_Final" title="1971 FA Cup Final">FA Cup Final</a> but, as in the Fairs Cup, experience was the major factor and Shankly's young team were beaten 2–1 by league champions Arsenal despite having taken the lead in extra time through a Steve Heighway goal.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Watching from the sidelines was another new player whom Shankly had recently signed from <a href="/wiki/Scunthorpe_United_F.C." title="Scunthorpe United F.C.">Scunthorpe United</a> for £35,000 on Twentyman's recommendation.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> This was <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Keegan" title="Kevin Keegan">Kevin Keegan</a> and he was such an important addition to the new Liverpool team that Shankly devoted an entire chapter of his autobiography to him entitled <i>A Boy Called Keegan</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Shankly summarised Keegan as "the inspiration of the new team".<sup id="cite_ref-S123_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S123-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Keegan's first season, 1971–72, Liverpool missed out on winning the League Championship by a single point, the title going to <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C." title="Derby County F.C.">Derby County</a>. Shankly maintained that Liverpool were denied a definite penalty in their crucial away match against Derby and then had a good goal disallowed towards the end of their final match against Arsenal.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Both decisions cost the team a vital point which would have been enough to claim first place. Shankly took encouragement from the team's overall form, especially as they made a strong finish to the season, and he was confident of success in 1972–73.<sup id="cite_ref-S125_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S125-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly had always been noted for his use of psychology, both to encourage his own players and to raise doubt in the minds of opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> One of his lasting innovations is the "THIS IS ANFIELD" plaque secured to the wall above the players' tunnel.<sup id="cite_ref-K126_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K126-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Coupled with the roar of the crowd, it was designed to intimidate.<sup id="cite_ref-K126_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K126-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-S126_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S126-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In the first match after it was erected, Liverpool defeated Newcastle United 5–0, despite an attempt by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Macdonald" title="Malcolm Macdonald">Malcolm Macdonald</a> to joke about the sign.<sup id="cite_ref-S126_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S126-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Shankly would try to boost the confidence of his own players by announcing that a key opponent was unfit. When Keegan was about to play against <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Moore" title="Bobby Moore">Bobby Moore</a> for the first time, Shankly told him that Moore had been out at a night club and was hung over. Afterwards, Keegan having produced an outstanding performance against the equally outstanding Moore, Shankly told him that Moore had been brilliant that day and Keegan would never play against anyone better.<sup id="cite_ref-K264_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K264-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
