</p><p>In 1972–73, Liverpool won the club's eighth league title and their third under Shankly.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> A massive bonus for the club was winning the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Cup</a>, the club's first European success.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1973_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="1973 UEFA Cup Final">two-legged final</a> they faced <a href="/wiki/Borussia_M%C3%B6nchengladbach" title="Borussia Mönchengladbach">Borussia Mönchengladbach</a>, whom Shankly rated the best team in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-S131_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S131-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The first leg at Anfield had to be played twice after an abandonment due to heavy rain which flooded the pitch.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Shankly had left John Toshack out of the team but then, having studied the Borussia defence, recalled him for the rematch the following night. Toshack used his height and heading ability to great effect and created two goals for Keegan as Liverpool won 3–0.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The second leg in Mönchengladbach was a different story as Borussia took an early 2–0 lead and Shankly admitted he thought the final was lost, but Liverpool held on to win the final by an aggregate score of 3–2.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> It was the first time an English club had won both the league title and a European trophy in the same season.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool were well beaten by <a href="/wiki/Red_Star_Belgrade" title="Red Star Belgrade">Red Star Belgrade</a> in the second round of the 1973–74 European Cup and lost out to Leeds in the League Championship, finishing second.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In the third round of the FA Cup, Liverpool had to score a late equaliser to draw 2–2 at home against lowly <a href="/wiki/Doncaster_Rovers_F.C." title="Doncaster Rovers F.C.">Doncaster Rovers</a> but recovered to win the replay and then go all the way to the final.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In what proved to be Shankly's last competitive game in charge, Liverpool produced a superb second half performance to defeat Newcastle 3–0 at Wembley.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_fans">Relationship with fans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Relationship with fans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shankly_Scarf_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/40/Shankly_Scarf_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shankly_Scarf_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bill Shankly during a lap of honour at Anfield in April 1973, wearing the scarf thrown to him by a Liverpool fan</div></div></div>
<p>In his autobiography, Shankly wrote: "Right from the start as a manager [i.e., when he was at Carlisle] I tried to show that the fans are the people that matter. You've got to know how to treat them (and) have them on your side".<sup id="cite_ref-S63_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S63-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> This was particularly true at Liverpool and Shankly said he was made for Liverpool where the people that matter most are the ones who come through the turnstiles.<sup id="cite_ref-S175_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S175-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> He added that a manager has got to identify himself with the people because their team is something that really matters to them. In return, he said, the support of the Liverpool fans for their team had been incredible.<sup id="cite_ref-S175_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S175-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1973, when Shankly and the team were showing off the League Championship trophy to the fans on <a href="/wiki/Spion_Kop_(stadiums)" title="Spion Kop (stadiums)">the Kop</a>, he saw a policeman fling aside a Liverpool scarf which had been thrown in Shankly's direction. Shankly retrieved the scarf and wore it. He said to the policeman: "Don't you do that. That's precious".<sup id="cite_ref-S138_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S138-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Shankly saw the offer of the scarf as a mark of respect which deserved his respect in return.<sup id="cite_ref-S138_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S138-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly emphasised the importance of communication with the supporters. At Carlisle he used to speak to them over the public address system before matches. Rather than just putting a few lines in the match programme, he preferred to speak and explain his team changes and his views about the previous match.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> At Workington, he would answer supporters' letters in person, using an old typewriter.<sup id="cite_ref-S70_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S70-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> He said he preferred to phone business people as he would put as little as possible in writing when dealing with them.<sup id="cite_ref-S70_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S70-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> He would readily obtain match tickets for fans whom he considered to be deserving cases and wrote in his autobiography that he would give people anything within reason.<sup id="cite_ref-S181_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S181-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly formed a special bond with the Liverpool supporters and, at the end of the 1961–62 season when Liverpool won the Second Division championship, he told the <i><a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Echo" title="Liverpool Echo">Liverpool Echo</a></i>: "In all sincerity, I can say that they are the greatest crowd of supporters in the game".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In Tommy Smith's view, Shankly was completely in tune with the city of Liverpool; he loved the supporters and they loved him, mainly because they knew he understood them.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_from_Liverpool">Retirement from Liverpool</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Retirement from Liverpool">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shankly was 60 when Liverpool won the 1974 FA Cup final and said in his autobiography that, on returning to the dressing room at the end of the match, he felt tired from all the years.<sup id="cite_ref-S141_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S141-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> His mind was made up and he knew he was going to retire.<sup id="cite_ref-S141_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S141-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> His wife, Nessie, had asked him to retire a year earlier but he decided that was not the right time.<sup id="cite_ref-S142_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S142-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Tommy Smith said that Shankly's feelings for Nessie were undoubtedly a major reason for his decision.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In 1974, he decided that he could leave Liverpool with pride in a job well done and only one regret, which was that he did not win the European Cup.<sup id="cite_ref-S142_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S142-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly had considered retirement in previous years. The Liverpool secretary Peter Robinson was initially blasé in 1974 but, when he realised Shankly was serious this time, tried to make him change his mind. Shankly's granddaughter, Karen Gill, said to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> in 2009: "I think that perhaps it was tiredness, that football had taken its toll on him".<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In her 2006 book about her grandfather, Karen Gill said Shankly never professed anything privately about retirement that he did not declare publicly. She did not agree that there was any hidden motive behind his decision and she thought Brian Clough's view about tiredness was probably correct.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly's retirement was officially and surprisingly announced at a press conference called by Liverpool on 12 July 1974. Chairman, John Smith, said in his opening address:<sup id="cite_ref-LFC-RET_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LFC-RET-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is with great regret that I as chairman of Liverpool Football Club have to inform you that Mr Shankly has intimated that he wishes to retire from active participation in league football. And the board has with extreme reluctance accepted his decision. I would like to at this stage place on record the board's great appreciation of Mr Shankly's magnificent achievements over the period of his managership.</p></blockquote>
<p>Shankly soon regretted his decision and tried to continue his involvement with the club, mainly by turning up for team training at Melwood. He said he still wanted the involvement as the club had become his life.<sup id="cite_ref-S143_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S143-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> He soon stopped going to Melwood because he felt there was some resentment and people were asking what he was doing there.<sup id="cite_ref-S144_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S144-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> He still attended matches, but sat in the stand away from the directors and staff. He was especially annoyed that Liverpool did not invite him to attend away matches as the club's guest. When, finally, he was invited to travel with them to the away leg of the <a href="/wiki/1976_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="1976 UEFA Cup Final">1976 UEFA Cup Final</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>, he was accommodated in a separate hotel and said he found that insulting.<sup id="cite_ref-S144_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S144-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Shankly contrasted Liverpool's attitude with what he encountered at other clubs, including Liverpool's great rivals Everton and Manchester United, where he was received warmly. He recalled Manchester United manager Tommy Docherty's comment to a Liverpool director that he (Shankly) was welcome at Old Trafford.<sup id="cite_ref-S145_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S145-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> About Everton, once his greatest rivals, he wrote that he had been received more warmly by Everton than by Liverpool. He said it was a scandal that he needed to say that about the club he had helped to build.<sup id="cite_ref-S145_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S145-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From Liverpool's point of view, the situation was that Shankly had retired and the club had to move on. Shankly did not understand that, by turning up for training at Melwood, he was effectively undermining Bob Paisley's status as manager. On one visit to Melwood, a player opined to Shankly that Paisley had make a good start in the job. Shankly retorted: "I could have left a monkey in charge!"<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Shankly's visits even went as far as actually taking over the training. Tommy Smith recalled that Shankly as manager never ran training and would only speak to Paisley, Fagan and Bennett about what needed to be done. As a visitor at Melwood, he began to intervene and Paisley's initial pleasure on seeing him soon turned to polite embarrassment. Eventually, Paisley had to point out to Shankly that he did not work there any more, that it was now Paisley's team and that he had things which he wanted to do with the team.<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is believed that Shankly wanted a seat on the Liverpool board, as Matt Busby had been given by Manchester United after he retired as their manager in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Again, what Shankly did not understand was that his relations with the board had often been acrimonious with several resignation threats and a statement made by Shankly that:<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>At a football club, there's a holy trinity – the players, the manager and the supporters. Directors don't come into it. They are only there to sign the cheques.</p></blockquote>
<p>Although some club officials like secretary Peter Robinson wanted bygones to be bygones, Tommy Smith summarised the board's view as one of satisfaction that Shankly had gone at last.<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> There was a perception that Shankly was an overbearing figure, who could use a position on the board to be a "back-seat driver", and the board were well aware that Matt Busby's time as a Manchester United director had been disastrous. Busby's successors had complained about being undermined by his continued presence at United, who had been relegated in <a href="/wiki/1973%E2%80%9374_in_English_football" title="1973–74 in English football">1974</a>. Whilst Liverpool's treatment of Shankly may have seemed disrespectful, they were acting in the best interests of the club and its new manager by pursuing the same relentless winning ethic that Shankly himself had instilled. In any event, their perceived ruthlessness was vindicated by the unprecedented haul of League Championship titles and European Cups won over the next decade<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> under Bob Paisley<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and Joe Fagan.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon after Shankly's retirement, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a> -- who himself had recently been sacked by <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United" class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds United">Leeds United</a> -- was asked during a <a href="/wiki/David_Frost" title="David Frost">David Frost</a> interview whom in football management he respected, to which Clough replied: "Well, the guy who had my total respect finished a few months ago at Liverpool. He's a one off, there'll never been another one like Shank. Never at all. He absolutely lives the game ... he was totally honest, he believed implicitly in what he was doing, and there was never, ever a doubt when you either talked to him, met him, or anything; he was above board. He was above board. He was one off."<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bill_Shankly_statue,_Anfield_2018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg/170px-Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg/255px-Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg/340px-Bill_Shankly_statue%2C_Anfield_2018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3340" data-file-height="4980" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bill_Shankly_statue,_Anfield_2018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue of Shankly, erected in 1997, outside the Kop at <a href="/wiki/Anfield" title="Anfield">Anfield</a></div></div></div>
<p>Shankly was awarded the <a href="/wiki/British_honours_system" class="mw-redirect" title="British honours system">OBE</a> in November 1974, four months after he retired as Liverpool manager. He and Nessie went to <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> and, according to Kelly, that was a rare day out for them.<sup id="cite_ref-K309_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K309-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> They continued to live in the semi-detached house at <a href="/wiki/West_Derby" title="West Derby">West Derby</a>, near the Everton training ground at <a href="/wiki/Bellefield" title="Bellefield">Bellefield</a>, which they bought when they moved to Liverpool in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-K306_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K306-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> After Shankly's death in 1981, Nessie lived there alone until she died in August 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly tried to keep busy in retirement and stay in touch with football. He worked for <a href="/wiki/Radio_City_96.7" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio City 96.7">Radio City 96.7</a>, a Liverpool station on which he presented his own chat show, once interviewing prime minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, and was a pundit on its football coverage. He briefly took up advisory roles at <a href="/wiki/Wrexham_A.F.C." title="Wrexham A.F.C.">Wrexham</a> and then at <a href="/wiki/Tranmere_Rovers_F.C." title="Tranmere Rovers F.C.">Tranmere Rovers</a>, helping former Liverpool captain Ron Yeats at the latter. In November 1976, the press speculated that Shankly would make a return to management as the successor to <a href="/wiki/Dave_Mackay" title="Dave Mackay">Dave Mackay</a> at Derby County, but the position went to <a href="/wiki/Colin_Murphy_(footballer,_born_1950)" title="Colin Murphy (footballer, born 1950)">Colin Murphy</a> instead. The following year Shankly privately expressed an interest in taking the Everton job, a move which would have caused a sensation in Football. Shankly stated “They’ve had enough of <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bingham" title="Billy Bingham">Billy Bingham</a> and they are looking for a new manager. <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Docherty" title="Tommy Docherty">Tommy Docherty</a> has let them know I could be interested and I tell you this, if they contact me and the offer is right I’ll take the job.” <sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> John Toshack recalled that Shankly was a great help to him when he went into management with <a href="/wiki/Swansea_City_A.F.C." title="Swansea City A.F.C.">Swansea City</a> in 1978. Despite being in his sixties, Shankly kept himself fit and often took part in five-a-side football. If nothing else was available he would join teams of youngsters in kickabouts.<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_legacy">Death and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Death and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On the morning of 26 September 1981, Bill Shankly was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Broadgreen_Hospital" title="Broadgreen Hospital">Broadgreen Hospital</a> following a heart attack. His condition appeared to be stable and there was no suggestion that his life was in danger. On the following Monday morning, his condition suddenly deteriorated and was transferred into intensive care. At 00:30 on 29 September, he suffered another cardiac arrest and was certified dead, aged 68, at 01:20. He was cremated at the <a href="/wiki/Anfield_Crematorium" class="mw-redirect" title="Anfield Crematorium">Anfield Crematorium</a> on 2 October and his ashes were scattered on the Anfield pitch at the Kop end.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the day of Shankly's death, training was cancelled at both Melwood and Bellefield.<sup id="cite_ref-K313_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K313-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> conference stood in a minute's silence for a man who had always been a socialist.<sup id="cite_ref-K313_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K313-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Sir Matt Busby, the former Manchester United manager, was so upset that he refused to take any telephone calls from people asking him for a reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-K313_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K313-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Tributes poured in from the world of football, especially from the former players of all Shankly's clubs. Liverpool chairman John Smith summed them up with a simple but fitting: "In my opinion, he was the most outstanding and dynamic manager of the century".<sup id="cite_ref-K313_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K313-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Liverpool erected the 15-foot high cast-iron Shankly Gates in front of the Anfield Road stand. Inscribed "You'll Never Walk Alone", they were opened by Nessie Shankly at a low-key ceremony in August 1982. In 1997, a seven-foot tall bronze statue of Shankly was unveiled outside the stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-G09_159-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G09-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> It bears the legend: "He made the people happy".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From the mid-1990s, Preston North End started a complete re-building of <a href="/wiki/Deepdale" title="Deepdale">Deepdale</a> to convert it into a modern all-seater stadium. When the former Spion Kop end was replaced by a new stand in 1998, it was named the Bill Shankly Kop and was designed with different coloured seats providing an image of Shankly's head and shoulders.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly was made an inaugural inductee of the <a href="/wiki/English_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="English Football Hall of Fame">English Football Hall of Fame</a> in 2002, in recognition of his impact on the English game as a manager.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Peace" title="David Peace">David Peace</a>'s biographical novel <a href="/wiki/Red_or_Dead_(novel)" title="Red or Dead (novel)"><i>Red or Dead</i></a>, published in 2013, is a fictionalised account of Shankly's career as Liverpool manager.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The novel was short-listed for the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Goldsmiths_Prize" title="Goldsmiths Prize">Goldsmiths Prize</a> (2013).<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> A hotel and museum opened in Liverpool in August 2015 dedicated to the life and career of Bill Shankly.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Stephen Kelly, in his 1997 biography of Shankly, calls him "the ultimate obsessive".<sup id="cite_ref-K3_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K3-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Shankly was fanatical about Liverpool and about football in general. From coaching his players and arguing with journalists to buttonholing fans in pubs, all Shankly ever wanted to do was talk about football and to be involved in football.<sup id="cite_ref-K3_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K3-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> As a result, he had few interests outside the game other than his family. His wife Nessie told Kelly that Shankly would spend time in the garden, mowing and weeding.<sup id="cite_ref-K293_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K293-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> She could rely on him to clean the cooker when Liverpool lost.<sup id="cite_ref-K293_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K293-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Family holidays were limited to a week in <a href="/wiki/Blackpool" title="Blackpool">Blackpool</a>, where they always stayed at the Norbreck Hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-K293_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K293-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> One non-football activity that Shankly did enjoy was playing cards and Ian St John said he loved it, always taking part on long coach trips to away matches.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly was noted for his personality and his wit; as a result, he is oft-quoted.<sup id="cite_ref-DMQ_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DMQ-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> His most famous quotation is probably one that is often misquoted:<sup id="cite_ref-DMQ_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DMQ-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Some people believe football is a matter of life and death, I am very disappointed with that attitude. I can assure you it is much, much more important than that.<sup id="cite_ref-DMQ_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DMQ-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Kelly wrote that, although it was said half-jokingly, so far as Shankly was concerned there was a degree of truth in what he had said. Shankly had fully realised the importance of football to its die-hard fans, himself included. It had become too important.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Ian St John agreed that much of Shankly's behaviour was "bizarre", but everything was done with a purpose because Shankly always knew what he was doing and what he was saying.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his autobiography, Shankly gave insights into his character such as his world-view as a socialist, explaining that the socialism he believed in was not about politics but about collectivism, with everyone working for each other and enjoying a share of the rewards. That was the basis of his approach to football which is a team game in which everyone works together and shares the rewards.<sup id="cite_ref-S32_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S32-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> He admitted to idolising <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a>, whose birthplace was only 26 miles from Glenbuck and he was inspired by many of Burns' philosophical quotations, such as <i><a href="/wiki/A_man%27s_a_man_for_a%27_that" class="mw-redirect" title="A man&#39;s a man for a&#39; that">A man's a man for a' that</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-S33_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S33-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Shankly had no time for bigotry or prejudice, especially arising from differences of religion. He compared the cities of Glasgow and Liverpool by saying that there is nothing like the <a href="/wiki/Old_Firm" title="Old Firm">Rangers–Celtic situation</a> in Liverpool because the supporters of Liverpool and Everton are a mixed bunch whose religion is football.<sup id="cite_ref-S31_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shankly's public persona was that of a "tough guy" with the swagger of his favourite film star <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a> but privately he was very different. Joe Mercer described his "heart of gold" and likened him to a <a href="/wiki/Border_Collie" title="Border Collie">Border Collie</a> who drives his sheep but could never hurt them.<sup id="cite_ref-K240_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K240-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Liverpool's perceived failings in the late 1960s have been attributed to Shankly's reluctance to drop his long-serving players even though they were past their best. Bob Paisley said it was Shankly's one failing and it was because he was "a softie at heart".<sup id="cite_ref-K240_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K240-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
When asked by a TV interviewer how he would like to be remembered, Shankly replied:<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p>Basically as an honest man in a game that is sometimes short on honesty. That I've been working honestly for the people of Liverpool to try and give them entertainment.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_player">As a player</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: As a player">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Preston North End</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a>: <a href="/wiki/1938_FA_Cup_Final" title="1938 FA Cup Final">1937–38</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_manager">As a manager</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: As a manager">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Liverpool</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">Football League First Division</a>: <a href="/wiki/1963%E2%80%9364_Football_League" title="1963–64 Football League">1963–64</a>, <a href="/wiki/1965%E2%80%9366_Football_League" title="1965–66 Football League">1965–66</a>, <a href="/wiki/1972%E2%80%9373_Football_League" title="1972–73 Football League">1972–73</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Football_League_Second_Division" title="Football League Second Division">Football League Second Division</a>: <a href="/wiki/1961%E2%80%9362_Football_League" title="1961–62 Football League">1961–62</a></li>
<li>FA Cup: <a href="/wiki/1965_FA_Cup_Final" title="1965 FA Cup Final">1964–65</a>, <a href="/wiki/1974_FA_Cup_Final" title="1974 FA Cup Final">1973–74</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/FA_Community_Shield" title="FA Community Shield">FA Charity Shield</a>: <a href="/wiki/1964_FA_Charity_Shield" title="1964 FA Charity Shield">1964</a>, <a href="/wiki/1965_FA_Charity_Shield" title="1965 FA Charity Shield">1965</a>, <a href="/wiki/1966_FA_Charity_Shield" title="1966 FA Charity Shield">1966</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Cup</a>: <a href="/wiki/1973_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="1973 UEFA Cup Final">1972–73</a></li></ul>
<p><b>Individual</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/LMA_Manager_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="LMA Manager of the Year">Manager of the Year Award</a>: <a href="/wiki/1972%E2%80%9373_Football_League" title="1972–73 Football League">1972–73</a><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Officer_of_the_Order_of_the_British_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Officer of the Order of the British Empire">Officer of the Order of the British Empire</a>: 1974<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/PFA_Merit_Award" title="PFA Merit Award">PFA Merit Award</a>: 1978<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/English_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="English Football Hall of Fame">Inaugural Inductee to the English Football Hall of Fame</a>: 2002</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scottish_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="Scottish Football Hall of Fame">Inaugural Inductee to the Scottish Football Hall of Fame</a>: 2004<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In April 2013, Shankly was honoured by <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> in the United Kingdom, as one of six people selected as subjects for the "Great Britons" <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_commemorative_stamps_2010%E2%80%9319" title="Great Britain commemorative stamps 2010–19">commemorative postage stamp</a> issue.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-wing-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-wing_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-wing_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The position of <a href="/wiki/Wing_half" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing half">wing half</a> is now obsolete in football terminology but it was an important role at the time of Shankly's career when teams routinely played in a <a href="/wiki/Formation_(association_football)#2–3–5" title="Formation (association football)">2–3–5 formation</a>. The wing halves (right and left) played outside the <a href="/wiki/Centre-half" class="mw-redirect" title="Centre-half">centre-half</a> in the middle three. Although some wing halves were more creative than defensive, Shankly's job was to win the ball (hence his emphasis on his tackling skills) and move it forward, so he was the equivalent of what is called a <a href="/wiki/Holding_midfielder" class="mw-redirect" title="Holding midfielder">holding midfielder</a> in 21st century football.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Shankly&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
